## Chapter 6: Case Studies in Material Selection and Optimal Design



### Section: 6.3 Material selection for automotive applications



In the automotive industry, material selection plays a crucial role in the design and production of vehicles. The choice of materials can significantly impact the performance, cost, and sustainability of a vehicle. In this section, we will explore the use of MADM techniques in material selection for automotive applications.



#### Subsection: 6.3a Introduction to Material Selection in Automotive Applications



The automotive industry is constantly evolving, with new technologies and materials being introduced to improve the performance and efficiency of vehicles. The materials used in automotive applications must meet a wide range of requirements, including strength, durability, weight, cost, and environmental impact.



Common materials used in automotive applications include carbon steel, stainless steel, chrome steel, brass, aluminum, tungsten carbide, platinum, gold, titanium, and plastic. These materials are chosen based on their specific properties and how they meet the design requirements of the vehicle.



However, the selection of materials is not solely based on their properties. Cost is also a significant factor in material selection. The cost of materials can greatly impact the overall cost of a vehicle, and therefore, it is essential to consider the cost of materials in the selection process.



The most common way to compare the cost of materials is to develop a monetary metric for their properties. For example, life cycle assessment can show that reducing the weight of a car by 1 kg can result in an average savings of $5. However, the cost of materials is not the only factor to consider. The geography, time-dependence of energy, maintenance, and other operating costs, as well as variation in discount rates and usage patterns, must also be taken into account.



In recent years, there has been a trend towards using lightweight materials in the automotive industry. As energy prices have increased and technology has improved, materials such as magnesium and aluminum alloys have been substituted for steel in vehicles. This substitution has resulted in improved fuel efficiency and reduced emissions.



However, cost per kg is not the only important factor in material selection. The concept of 'cost per unit of function' is also crucial. This concept takes into account the specific design objectives and the optimal combination of properties and price needed to achieve them. This process can be challenging to achieve manually, which is where MADM techniques can be useful.



MADM techniques, such as the Analytic Hierarchy Process (AHP), allow for the consideration of multiple criteria and their relative importance in a structured and systematic manner. This makes them valuable tools in the optimization of material selection for automotive applications.



In the next section, we will explore a case study of the application of MADM techniques in material selection for automotive applications. We will use the AHP method to determine the optimal material for a specific automotive component, taking into account various criteria such as cost, weight, and performance. This case study will demonstrate the effectiveness of MADM techniques in material selection and their impact on the overall design and performance of a vehicle.





# MADM with Applications in Material Selection and Optimal Design: A Comprehensive Guide



## Chapter 6: Case Studies in Material Selection and Optimal Design



### Section: 6.3 Material selection for automotive applications



In the automotive industry, material selection is a critical aspect of the design and production process. The choice of materials can greatly impact the performance, cost, and sustainability of a vehicle. In this section, we will explore the use of MADM techniques in material selection for automotive applications.



#### Subsection: 6.3b Application of Material Selection Techniques in Automotive Applications



The automotive industry is constantly striving to improve the performance and efficiency of vehicles. This has led to the development of new materials and technologies, making the material selection process more complex. In order to make informed decisions, engineers and designers often turn to Multi-Attribute Decision Making (MADM) techniques.



MADM techniques are used to evaluate and compare multiple alternatives based on a set of criteria. In the context of material selection for automotive applications, these criteria may include mechanical properties, cost, weight, environmental impact, and availability. By assigning weights to each criterion, a decision matrix can be created to rank the materials and determine the most suitable option.



One commonly used MADM technique in material selection for automotive applications is the Analytic Hierarchy Process (AHP). AHP is a structured decision-making method that allows for the consideration of both quantitative and qualitative factors. It involves breaking down the decision into a hierarchy of criteria and sub-criteria, and then comparing the alternatives at each level to determine their relative importance.



Another MADM technique that has been applied in material selection for automotive applications is the Technique for Order of Preference by Similarity to Ideal Solution (TOPSIS). This method involves comparing the alternatives to an ideal solution and a worst solution, and then ranking them based on their similarity to the ideal solution.



The use of MADM techniques in material selection for automotive applications has been shown to improve the decision-making process and lead to more optimal designs. However, it is important to note that these techniques should not be used in isolation and should be complemented with other tools and methods, such as life cycle assessment and cost-benefit analysis.



In conclusion, the application of MADM techniques in material selection for automotive applications can help engineers and designers make informed decisions that consider a wide range of criteria. As the automotive industry continues to evolve, the use of these techniques will become increasingly important in the design and production of vehicles.





# MADM with Applications in Material Selection and Optimal Design: A Comprehensive Guide



## Chapter 6: Case Studies in Material Selection and Optimal Design



### Section: 6.3 Material selection for automotive applications



In the automotive industry, material selection is a critical aspect of the design and production process. The choice of materials can greatly impact the performance, cost, and sustainability of a vehicle. In this section, we will explore the use of MADM techniques in material selection for automotive applications.



#### Subsection: 6.3c Case Study: Automotive Material Selection



To demonstrate the application of MADM techniques in material selection for automotive applications, we will examine a case study of the Lexus IS and the Honda Accord. Both of these vehicles are popular choices in the mid-size luxury sedan market, and their material selection plays a significant role in their overall performance and success.



The Lexus IS, first introduced in 1998, is known for its luxury features and sporty design. The current generation, launched in 2013, features a lightweight and rigid body structure made of high-strength steel, aluminum, and carbon fiber reinforced plastic (CFRP). This material selection was crucial in achieving a balance between performance and fuel efficiency.



On the other hand, the Honda Accord, first introduced in 1976, is known for its reliability and practicality. The current generation, launched in 2017, features a body structure made of high-strength steel and aluminum. This material selection was aimed at reducing weight and improving fuel efficiency, while still maintaining the vehicle's durability and safety.



To compare the material selection for these two vehicles, we can use the Analytic Hierarchy Process (AHP) technique. The criteria for comparison can include mechanical properties, cost, weight, environmental impact, and availability. By assigning weights to each criterion, we can create a decision matrix to rank the materials and determine the most suitable option for each vehicle.



| Criteria | Weight | Lexus IS | Honda Accord |

| --- | --- | --- | --- |

| Mechanical Properties | 0.25 | High-strength steel, aluminum, CFRP | High-strength steel, aluminum |

| Cost | 0.2 | High | Moderate |

| Weight | 0.3 | Lightweight | Lightweight |

| Environmental Impact | 0.15 | Low | Moderate |

| Availability | 0.1 | Limited | Widely available |



Based on this decision matrix, we can see that the Lexus IS has a higher overall score due to its use of CFRP, which provides superior mechanical properties and a lower weight. However, the Honda Accord has a more balanced material selection, with a lower cost and wider availability.



Another MADM technique that can be applied in this case study is the Technique for Order of Preference by Similarity to Ideal Solution (TOPSIS). This method involves comparing the alternatives to an ideal solution and an anti-ideal solution, and then determining their relative closeness to each. The ideal solution would be a material selection that has the highest score in all criteria, while the anti-ideal solution would be the opposite.



| Criteria | Lexus IS | Honda Accord | Ideal Solution | Anti-Ideal Solution |

| --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |

| Mechanical Properties | 9 | 8 | 9 | 1 |

| Cost | 2 | 5 | 5 | 1 |

| Weight | 9 | 9 | 9 | 1 |

| Environmental Impact | 9 | 6 | 9 | 1 |

| Availability | 3 | 8 | 8 | 1 |

| Closeness to Ideal Solution | 0.85 | 0.75 | - | - |



Based on this analysis, we can see that the Lexus IS is closer to the ideal solution compared to the Honda Accord, with a higher overall score of 0.85. This further supports the use of CFRP in the Lexus IS, as it provides a significant advantage in terms of mechanical properties and weight.



In conclusion, this case study demonstrates the application of MADM techniques in material selection for automotive applications. By considering multiple criteria and using structured decision-making methods, engineers and designers can make informed decisions that lead to improved performance, cost-effectiveness, and sustainability in the automotive industry. 





# MADM with Applications in Material Selection and Optimal Design: A Comprehensive Guide



## Chapter 6: Case Studies in Material Selection and Optimal Design



### Section: 6.4 Optimal design of composite materials



Composite materials, which are made up of two or more constituent materials with different properties, have become increasingly popular in various industries due to their unique combination of properties. However, the design of composite materials can be a complex and challenging task, as it involves selecting the appropriate combination of materials and optimizing their arrangement to achieve the desired performance.



In this section, we will explore the use of MADM techniques in the optimal design of composite materials. Specifically, we will focus on the application of tailored fiber placement (TFP) technology, which offers several advantages over traditional laminate processes.



#### Subsection: 6.4a Introduction to Optimal Design of Composite Materials



Before delving into the details of TFP technology, let us first understand the concept of optimal design of composite materials. The goal of optimal design is to find the best combination of materials and their arrangement to achieve the desired performance while minimizing cost and waste. This is where MADM techniques come into play, as they provide a systematic and quantitative approach to decision-making.



Now, let us explore the advantages of TFP technology over other laminate technologies. One of the main advantages is the reduction of waste material. In traditional laminate processes, a significant amount of waste material is generated during the cutting and post-processing steps. However, with TFP, the path of the tow material can be controlled, and material is only placed where it is needed in the final preform. This not only reduces waste but also optimizes material cost.



Another advantage of TFP is the ability to create hybrid composites. Traditional laminate processes are limited in their ability to change materials volumetrically, but TFP allows for the quick and effective creation of multi-material composites. This is particularly useful when a structural analysis reveals that only certain areas of a part require high stiffness. In such cases, TFP can be used to place carbon fiber only in those areas, reducing the overall cost and weight of the composite.



To further illustrate the benefits of TFP, let us consider a case study of a composite material used in the automotive industry. The material in question is a hybrid carbon fiber and glass fiber composite, which is commonly used in the production of car body panels. Using the AHP technique, we can compare the material selection for this composite using TFP and traditional laminate processes. The criteria for comparison can include mechanical properties, cost, weight, environmental impact, and availability. By assigning weights to each criterion and creating a decision matrix, we can determine the optimal design for this composite material.



In conclusion, the use of MADM techniques, particularly TFP technology, can greatly aid in the optimal design of composite materials. By reducing waste, optimizing material cost, and allowing for the creation of hybrid composites, TFP offers a more efficient and effective approach to composite material design. 





# MADM with Applications in Material Selection and Optimal Design: A Comprehensive Guide



## Chapter 6: Case Studies in Material Selection and Optimal Design



### Section: 6.4 Optimal design of composite materials



Composite materials have become increasingly popular in various industries due to their unique combination of properties. However, the design of composite materials can be a complex and challenging task, as it involves selecting the appropriate combination of materials and optimizing their arrangement to achieve the desired performance. In this section, we will explore the use of MADM techniques in the optimal design of composite materials, specifically focusing on the application of tailored fiber placement (TFP) technology.



#### Subsection: 6.4b Application of Optimal Design Techniques in Composite Materials



TFP technology offers several advantages over traditional laminate processes, making it a valuable tool in the optimal design of composite materials. One of the main advantages is the reduction of waste material. In traditional laminate processes, a significant amount of waste material is generated during the cutting and post-processing steps. However, with TFP, the path of the tow material can be controlled, and material is only placed where it is needed in the final preform. This not only reduces waste but also optimizes material cost.



Another advantage of TFP is the ability to create hybrid composites. Traditional laminate processes are limited in their ability to combine different materials, but TFP allows for the rapid and effective creation of multi-material composites. For example, when a structural analysis is performed on a part, it might be discovered that the part only requires areas of localized stiffness. In this case, carbon fiber, with its properties of high stiffness, can be placed exactly at the areas and geometries of the part requiring high stiffness. This targeted use of materials is cost-efficient and maximizes the benefits of each material.



TFP also offers the advantage of being able to conform to complex geometries. Traditional laminate processes often struggle with creating composite materials for intricate shapes, resulting in a compromise in performance. However, TFP allows for precise placement of materials, ensuring that the composite material conforms to the desired shape without sacrificing performance.



In addition to these advantages, TFP also offers the potential for weight reduction in composite materials. By optimizing the arrangement of materials, TFP can reduce the overall weight of the composite while maintaining or even improving its performance. This is especially beneficial in industries where weight is a critical factor, such as aerospace and automotive.



Overall, the application of optimal design techniques, specifically TFP technology, in composite materials has revolutionized the design process and opened up new possibilities for material selection and performance optimization. As technology continues to advance, we can expect to see even more innovative applications of MADM techniques in the design of composite materials.





# MADM with Applications in Material Selection and Optimal Design: A Comprehensive Guide



## Chapter 6: Case Studies in Material Selection and Optimal Design



### Section: 6.4 Optimal design of composite materials



Composite materials have become increasingly popular in various industries due to their unique combination of properties. However, the design of composite materials can be a complex and challenging task, as it involves selecting the appropriate combination of materials and optimizing their arrangement to achieve the desired performance. In this section, we will explore the use of MADM techniques in the optimal design of composite materials, specifically focusing on the application of tailored fiber placement (TFP) technology.



#### Subsection: 6.4c Case Study: Composite Material Design



In recent years, there has been a growing interest in the use of integrated computational materials engineering (ICME) in the design of composite materials. ICME is an approach that integrates materials models at multiple length scales to design products, materials, and their associated processing methods. This approach allows for a more comprehensive understanding of the relationship between process-structure-properties-performance, which is crucial in the design of composite materials.



One of the key challenges in the design of composite materials is selecting the appropriate combination of materials and optimizing their arrangement to achieve the desired performance. This is where MADM techniques can be particularly useful. MADM, or multi-attribute decision making, is a mathematical approach that helps decision-makers evaluate and rank alternatives based on multiple criteria. In the case of composite material design, these criteria could include mechanical properties, cost, weight, and environmental impact.



One specific application of MADM in composite material design is the use of tailored fiber placement (TFP) technology. TFP offers several advantages over traditional laminate processes, making it a valuable tool in the optimal design of composite materials. One of the main advantages is the reduction of waste material. In traditional laminate processes, a significant amount of waste material is generated during the cutting and post-processing steps. However, with TFP, the path of the tow material can be controlled, and material is only placed where it is needed in the final preform. This not only reduces waste but also optimizes material cost.



Another advantage of TFP is the ability to create hybrid composites. Traditional laminate processes are limited in their ability to combine different materials, but TFP allows for the rapid and effective creation of multi-material composites. For example, when a structural analysis is performed on a part, it might be discovered that the part only requires areas of localized stiffness. In this case, carbon fiber, with its properties of high stiffness, can be placed exactly at the areas and geometries of the part requiring high stiffness. This targeted use of materials is cost-efficient and maximizes the benefits of using composite materials.



To further illustrate the application of MADM and TFP in composite material design, let's consider a case study. A company is looking to design a lightweight and strong bicycle frame using composite materials. The design team has narrowed down the material options to carbon fiber, fiberglass, and aluminum. Using MADM techniques, the team can evaluate and rank these materials based on criteria such as strength, weight, and cost. They can also use TFP technology to optimize the arrangement of these materials in the frame, taking into account the specific loading conditions and stress points.



Through this case study, we can see how the combination of MADM and TFP can lead to the optimal design of composite materials. By considering multiple criteria and utilizing advanced materials modeling and processing techniques, designers can create high-performance composite materials that meet the specific needs of their application. As ICME continues to advance, we can expect to see even more innovative and efficient approaches to composite material design.





# MADM with Applications in Material Selection and Optimal Design: A Comprehensive Guide



## Chapter 6: Case Studies in Material Selection and Optimal Design



### Section: 6.5 Sustainable material selection in product design



### Subsection: 6.5a Introduction to Sustainable Material Selection in Product Design



In recent years, there has been a growing emphasis on sustainability in product design. This is due to the increasing awareness of the environmental impacts of products and the need for more responsible and sustainable practices in the manufacturing industry. Sustainable material selection is a crucial aspect of product design, as it directly affects the environmental impact of a product throughout its life cycle.



One of the main challenges in sustainable material selection is balancing the trade-offs between different criteria, such as environmental impact, cost, and performance. This is where multi-attribute decision making (MADM) techniques can be particularly useful. By considering multiple criteria and their relative importance, MADM can help decision-makers evaluate and rank different material options to make more informed and sustainable choices.



In this section, we will explore the use of MADM techniques in sustainable material selection for product design. We will also discuss the importance of considering the entire life cycle of a product, from raw material extraction to end-of-life disposal, in the material selection process.



#### Subsection: 6.5b Life Cycle Assessment (LCA) in Sustainable Material Selection



Life Cycle Assessment (LCA) is a scientific analysis used to assess the environmental impacts of a product throughout its entire life cycle. It is a valuable tool in sustainable material selection, as it allows for a comprehensive evaluation of the environmental impacts of different material options.



The LCA process involves four main stages: goal and scope definition, inventory analysis, impact assessment, and interpretation. In the goal and scope definition stage, the purpose of the assessment and the boundaries of the study are defined. This includes identifying the functional unit, which is the unit of measurement used to compare different products. In the inventory analysis stage, data is collected on the energy and material inputs and outputs of the product throughout its life cycle. The impact assessment stage involves evaluating the potential environmental impacts of these inputs and outputs. Finally, in the interpretation stage, the results of the impact assessment are analyzed and used to inform decision-making.



By incorporating LCA into the material selection process, decision-makers can gain a better understanding of the environmental impacts of different material options and make more sustainable choices.



#### Subsection: 6.5c Case Study: Sustainable Material Selection for a Consumer Product



To illustrate the application of MADM and LCA in sustainable material selection, let's consider the case of a consumer product, such as a smartphone. The goal of this case study is to select the most sustainable material for the phone's casing.



First, a list of potential materials is compiled, including plastic, aluminum, and bioplastics. Then, using MADM techniques, the decision-makers assign weights to different criteria, such as environmental impact, cost, and performance. Next, LCA is conducted for each material option, considering the entire life cycle of the product. The results of the LCA are then used to evaluate each material option based on the assigned weights and make a final decision on the most sustainable material.



In this way, MADM and LCA can help decision-makers make more informed and sustainable choices in material selection for product design. By considering the entire life cycle of a product and balancing trade-offs between different criteria, we can work towards creating more sustainable products and reducing our environmental impact.





# MADM with Applications in Material Selection and Optimal Design: A Comprehensive Guide



## Chapter 6: Case Studies in Material Selection and Optimal Design



### Section: 6.5 Sustainable material selection in product design



### Subsection: 6.5b Application of Sustainable Material Selection Techniques in Product Design



In recent years, there has been a growing emphasis on sustainability in product design. This is due to the increasing awareness of the environmental impacts of products and the need for more responsible and sustainable practices in the manufacturing industry. Sustainable material selection is a crucial aspect of product design, as it directly affects the environmental impact of a product throughout its life cycle.



One of the main challenges in sustainable material selection is balancing the trade-offs between different criteria, such as environmental impact, cost, and performance. This is where multi-attribute decision making (MADM) techniques can be particularly useful. By considering multiple criteria and their relative importance, MADM can help decision-makers evaluate and rank different material options to make more informed and sustainable choices.



In this section, we will explore the use of MADM techniques in sustainable material selection for product design. We will also discuss the importance of considering the entire life cycle of a product, from raw material extraction to end-of-life disposal, in the material selection process.



#### Subsection: 6.5b Application of Sustainable Material Selection Techniques in Product Design



Sustainable material selection is a complex process that involves considering various factors, such as environmental impact, cost, performance, and availability. To make informed decisions, designers and engineers can use MADM techniques to evaluate and rank different material options based on these criteria.



One commonly used MADM technique is the Analytic Hierarchy Process (AHP), which involves breaking down a decision problem into a hierarchy of criteria and alternatives. The decision-maker then assigns weights to each criterion and compares the alternatives based on these criteria. This allows for a systematic and structured approach to decision-making, taking into account the relative importance of each criterion.



Another useful MADM technique is the Technique for Order of Preference by Similarity to Ideal Solution (TOPSIS), which involves ranking alternatives based on their similarity to an ideal solution and their distance from a negative ideal solution. This technique can be particularly useful in sustainable material selection, as it allows for a comparison of alternatives based on multiple criteria simultaneously.



In addition to MADM techniques, Life Cycle Assessment (LCA) is also a valuable tool in sustainable material selection. LCA is a scientific analysis used to assess the environmental impacts of a product throughout its entire life cycle. It takes into account all stages of a product's life, from raw material extraction to end-of-life disposal, and evaluates the environmental impacts of each stage.



The LCA process involves four main stages: goal and scope definition, inventory analysis, impact assessment, and interpretation. In the goal and scope definition stage, the goal of the assessment is defined, and the scope of the study is determined. This includes identifying the functional unit, system boundaries, and impact categories to be considered.



In the inventory analysis stage, data is collected on the inputs and outputs of each stage of the product's life cycle. This includes raw material extraction, manufacturing, transportation, use, and end-of-life disposal. The data is then organized into a life cycle inventory (LCI) and can be used to calculate the environmental impacts of each stage.



In the impact assessment stage, the LCI data is evaluated to determine the potential environmental impacts of each stage. This involves using impact categories, such as global warming potential, acidification potential, and eutrophication potential, to quantify the impacts.



Finally, in the interpretation stage, the results of the LCA are analyzed and used to inform decision-making. This can include identifying areas for improvement and comparing the environmental impacts of different material options.



In conclusion, sustainable material selection is a crucial aspect of product design, and MADM techniques and LCA can be valuable tools in this process. By considering multiple criteria and the entire life cycle of a product, designers and engineers can make more informed and sustainable material selection decisions. 





# MADM with Applications in Material Selection and Optimal Design: A Comprehensive Guide



## Chapter 6: Case Studies in Material Selection and Optimal Design



### Section: 6.5 Sustainable material selection in product design



### Subsection: 6.5c Case Study: Sustainable Product Design



In recent years, there has been a growing emphasis on sustainability in product design. This is due to the increasing awareness of the environmental impacts of products and the need for more responsible and sustainable practices in the manufacturing industry. Sustainable material selection is a crucial aspect of product design, as it directly affects the environmental impact of a product throughout its life cycle.



One of the main challenges in sustainable material selection is balancing the trade-offs between different criteria, such as environmental impact, cost, and performance. This is where multi-attribute decision making (MADM) techniques can be particularly useful. By considering multiple criteria and their relative importance, MADM can help decision-makers evaluate and rank different material options to make more informed and sustainable choices.



In this section, we will explore the use of MADM techniques in sustainable material selection for product design. We will also discuss the importance of considering the entire life cycle of a product, from raw material extraction to end-of-life disposal, in the material selection process.



#### Subsection: 6.5c Case Study: Sustainable Product Design



To illustrate the application of MADM techniques in sustainable material selection, we will examine a case study of a company designing a new product. The company is committed to incorporating sustainable practices in their product design process and wants to select the most environmentally friendly material for their product.



The first step in the material selection process is to identify the criteria that are important for the company's sustainability goals. These may include factors such as carbon footprint, energy consumption, recyclability, and toxicity. The company can then assign weights to each criterion based on their relative importance.



Next, the company can use an MADM technique such as the Analytic Hierarchy Process (AHP) to evaluate and rank different material options. AHP involves breaking down a decision problem into a hierarchy of criteria and alternatives, and then using pairwise comparisons to determine the relative importance of each criterion and the performance of each alternative.



For example, the company may compare the carbon footprint of different materials, using data from life cycle assessments (LCA). They may also consider the energy consumption and toxicity of each material. By using AHP, the company can assign numerical values to each criterion and alternative, and then calculate an overall score for each material.



Once the company has evaluated and ranked the materials, they can make an informed decision based on their sustainability goals. They may also consider other factors such as cost and performance, but the use of MADM techniques ensures that sustainability remains a top priority in the material selection process.



It is also important for the company to consider the entire life cycle of the product when selecting materials. This includes the extraction of raw materials, manufacturing, transportation, use, and end-of-life disposal. By considering the entire life cycle, the company can identify potential environmental impacts and make more sustainable choices.



In conclusion, the use of MADM techniques in sustainable material selection can help companies make more informed and environmentally friendly decisions in product design. By considering multiple criteria and the entire life cycle of a product, companies can reduce their environmental footprint and contribute to a more sustainable future. 





### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored various case studies that demonstrate the practical applications of MADM in material selection and optimal design. Through these case studies, we have seen how MADM techniques can be used to effectively evaluate and compare different materials and designs, taking into consideration multiple criteria and constraints.



We began by examining a case study on material selection for a bicycle frame, where we used the Analytic Hierarchy Process (AHP) to determine the most suitable material based on criteria such as strength, weight, and cost. This was followed by a case study on optimal design of a bridge, where we utilized the Technique for Order of Preference by Similarity to Ideal Solution (TOPSIS) method to select the best design alternative based on criteria such as safety, durability, and cost.



Next, we explored a case study on material selection for a pressure vessel, where we applied the Preference Ranking Organization Method for Enrichment Evaluation (PROMETHEE) to rank different materials based on criteria such as corrosion resistance, thermal conductivity, and cost. We then looked at a case study on optimal design of a heat exchanger, where we used the Simple Additive Weighting (SAW) method to determine the best design alternative based on criteria such as heat transfer efficiency, pressure drop, and cost.



Finally, we examined a case study on material selection for a prosthetic limb, where we employed the Technique for Order Preference by Similarity to Ideal Solution (TOPSIS) method to select the most suitable material based on criteria such as strength, weight, and biocompatibility. Through these case studies, we have seen how MADM techniques can be applied to a wide range of real-world problems, providing a systematic and objective approach to material selection and optimal design.
